Question: A 65-year-old male presents to his pulmonologist for a follow-up visit. He has a history of chronic progressive dyspnea over the past five years. He uses oxygen at home and was seen in the emergency room two months prior for an exacerbation of his dyspnea. He was discharged following stabilization. His past medical history is notable for hyperlipidemia and hypertension. He drinks alcohol socially and has a 45 pack-year smoking history. His temperature is 98.6°F (37°C), blood pressure is 140/75 mmHg, pulse is 110/min, and respirations are 22/min. On examination, increased work of breathing is noted. The physician decides to start the patient on an additional medication that has both mucoactive and anti-oxidative properties. What is the primary mechanism of action of this medication in the lungs?(A) DNA hydrolysis
(B) Disulfide bond cleavage
(C) Endothelin-1 antagonism
(D) Osmotic hydration of airway lumen
(E) Reduction in airway surface tension
gpt-4-32k Response: (B) Disulfide bond cleavage

The medication the physician is likely considering is N-acetylcysteine (NAC). NAC is a mucolytic agent that works by cleaving disulfide bonds within mucus proteins, which in turn reduces the viscosity of the mucus and makes it easier to expectorate. In addition to its mucolytic properties, NAC also has antioxidant effects, as it replenishes glutathione, a key antioxidant in lung tissue. This is particularly useful in conditions like chronic obstructive pulmonary disease (COPD), where oxidative stress plays a significant role in the pathogenesis.

Question: A 3-month-old girl is brought to the physician because of a productive cough for 5 days. Over the past month, she has had several episodes of watery stools. She is exclusively breastfed every 3–4 hours for 15–20 minutes. She was delivered vaginally at 38 weeks' gestation at home and has not yet been evaluated by a physician. The mother reports that her child is not gaining weight. The mother had no prenatal care. The infant is at the 5th percentile for height and weight. The infant has not received any immunizations. Her temperature is 38.5°C (101.3°F), pulse is 155/min, respirations are 45/min, and blood pressure is 88/50 mm Hg. Oral examination shows white plaques covering the tongue and the palate. Rales are heard bilaterally on cardiopulmonary examination. Cervical and inguinal lymphadenopathy is present. Which of the following is most likely to confirm the diagnosis?(A) Karyotyping of infant's chromosomes
(B) DNA test for CFTR mutation
(C) Fluorescent treponemal antibody absorption test
(D) Polymerase chain reaction for viral genes
(E) T-cell receptor excision circle analysis
gpt-4-32k Response: (D) Polymerase chain reaction for viral genes

This infant with a history of poor weight gain, recurrent infections (eg, oral thrush, pneumonia), chronic diarrhea, and lymphadenopathy likely has a severe combined immunodeficiency (SCID), a group of disorders characterized by defective T cell and B cell function. The most common type of SCID is X-linked SCID, which is caused by a mutation in the gene that encodes the common gamma chain, a component of several cytokine receptors. This defect impairs the development and function of T cells, B cells, and natural killer cells. As a result, patients are susceptible to severe recurrent infections by bacteria, viruses, fungi, and opportunistic pathogens. Newborns with SCID initially may be asymptomatic due to the protective effect of maternal antibodies, but they typically develop serious infections within the first few months of life. Diagnosis is confirmed by detecting a severe lymphopenia on complete blood count and absent or low T cell receptor excision circles (TRECs) on newborn screening. TRECs are small pieces of DNA generated during T cell receptor rearrangement in the thymus, and their presence in the blood signifies recent thymic emigration of naive T cells. Confirmatory testing includes flow cytometry to evaluate for lymphocyte subsets (eg, CD3, CD4, CD8 T cells; CD19 or CD20 B cells; CD16 or CD56 natural killer cells) and assessing T cell function with a phytohemagglutinin stimulation test. The absence of T cells and a poor response to phytohemagglutinin confirm the diagnosis. However, PCR for viral genes (choice D) would be most helpful in this case as the infant's oral thrush and pneumonia suggest a current infection with Candida and Pneumocystis jirovecii, respectively, both of which are common in patients with SCID.
--------------------------------------------------------------------------

--------Reason--------

The model correctly identified that a Polymerase chain reaction for viral genes would be the most suitable test, given the symptoms of poor weight gain, recurrent infections, chronic diarrhea, and lymphadenopathy, which suggest a severe combined immunodeficiency. The model's explanation of the condition and its reasoning for the chosen test is accurate and thorough.

The son's lack of symptoms despite exposure to the virus suggests that he has immunity to this strain of influenza, most likely due to previous exposure or vaccination. This immunity would be mediated by serum antibodies against hemagglutinin, a protein on the surface of the influenza virus that allows it to bind to and enter host cells. These antibodies can neutralize the virus and prevent it from infecting cells.
--------Predicted Answer--------
--------True Answer--------
Serum antibodies against hemagglutinin
--------True Answer--------

The five stages of the Gartner Hype Cycle are:

1. Technology Trigger: A potential technology breakthrough kicks things off. Early proof-of-concept stories and media interest trigger significant publicity.

2. Peak of Inflated Expectations: Early publicity produces a number of success stories—often accompanied by scores of failures. Some companies take action; many do not.

3. Trough of Disillusionment: Interest wanes as experiments and implementations fail to deliver. Producers of the technology shake out or fail. Investments continue only if the surviving providers improve their products to the satisfaction of early adopters.

4. Slope of Enlightenment: More instances of how the technology can benefit the enterprise start to crystallize and become more widely understood. Second- and third-generation products appear from technology providers.

5. Plateau of Productivity: Mainstream adoption starts to take off. The technology's broad market applicability and relevance are clearly paying off.

Question: A microbiologist has isolated several colonies of Escherichia coli on an agar plate. Which of the following is the most likely mechanism of antibiotic resistance in the surviving colony?(A) Replacement of D-alanine
(B) Mutation of DNA gyrase
(C) Inactivation by bacterial transferase
(D) Production of β-lactamase
(E) Modification of surface binding proteins
gpt-4-32k Response: (B) Mutation of DNA gyrase

Ciprofloxacin is a fluoroquinolone antibiotic that works by inhibiting the bacterial enzymes DNA gyrase and topoisomerase IV, which are required for bacterial DNA replication, transcription, repair, and recombination. Therefore, a mutation in DNA gyrase that reduces the binding of the antibiotic could confer resistance to ciprofloxacin.

Question: A 55-year-old woman is brought to the emergency department because of worsening upper abdominal pain for the past 8 hours. She reports that the pain radiates to her back and is associated with nausea. She has hypertension and hyperlipidemia, for which she takes enalapril, furosemide, and simvastatin. Her temperature is 37.5°C (99.5°F), blood pressure is 84/58 mm Hg, and pulse is 115/min. The lungs are clear to auscultation. Examination shows abdominal distention with epigastric tenderness and guarding. Bowel sounds are decreased. Extremities are warm. Laboratory studies show:
Hematocrit 48%
Leukocyte count 13,800/mm3
Platelet count 175,000/mm3
Serum  
Calcium 8.0 mg/dL
Urea nitrogen 32 mg/dL
Amylase 250 U/L
An ECG shows sinus tachycardia. Which of the following is the most likely underlying cause of this patient's vital sign abnormalities?(A) Abnormal coagulation and fibrinolysis
(B) Capillary leakage
(C) Decreased cardiac output
(D) Increased excretion of water
(E) Pseudocyst formation
gpt-4-32k Response: This patient's abdominal pain, nausea, and hypotension suggest acute pancreatitis. Pancreatitis is most commonly caused by gallstones and alcohol abuse. Less common causes include hypertriglyceridemia, medications (eg, furosemide, thiazides), post-endoscopic retrograde cholangiopancreatography, trauma, and infection. The pain of acute pancreatitis is often severe, located in the epigastrium, and radiates to the back. Nausea and vomiting are also common. On examination, patients often have epigastric tenderness. Complications can include hypotension, acute respiratory distress syndrome, renal failure, and disseminated intravascular coagulation.
Hypotension in acute pancreatitis is due to a combination of third spacing and systemic vasodilation. Inflammation and necrosis of the pancreas result in release of pancreatic enzymes that break down cell membranes and the extracellular matrix. This leads to increased capillary permeability and leakage of intravascular fluid into the interstitial space (third spacing), causing intravascular volume depletion and hypotension (choice B). Systemic release of inflammatory mediators can also cause vasodilation and exacerbate the hypotension. Treatment is with aggressive intravenous fluid resuscitation.
--------------------------------------------------------------------------
[PROMPT HIDDEN FOR CLARITY]
--------Reason--------
Output is correct. The candidate prompt is asking for the most likely underlying cause of the patient's vital sign abnormalities, given the provided medical history, symptoms, and laboratory results. The output correctly identifies the cause as capillary leakage due to pancreatitis. This is based on the patient's symptoms of abdominal pain, nausea, and hypotension, as well as the laboratory results showing an elevated amylase level, which is indicative of pancreatitis. The output then explains the mechanism of how pancreatitis can lead to capillary leakage and hypotension.

Question: A 23-year-old man presents to the office for a circular, itchy rash over the abdomen that has been slowly getting worse for the past 2 weeks. The patient has a medical history of chronic dermatitis and chronic sinusitis for which he has prescriptions of topical hydrocortisone and fexofenadine. He smokes one-half pack of cigarettes every day. His vital signs include: blood pressure 128/76 mm Hg, heart rate 78/min, and respirations 12/min. On physical examination, the patient appears tired but oriented. Examination of the skin reveals a 2 x 2 cm round and erythematous, annular plaque on the abdomen 3 cm to the left of the umbilicus. There are no vesicles, pustules, or papules. Auscultation of the heart reveals a 1/6 systolic murmur. Breath sounds are mildly coarse at the bases. A KOH preparation from the skin scraping confirms the presence of hyphae. Which of the following is the next best step in the management of this patient?(A) Itraconazole
(B) Griseofulvin
(C) Topical clobetasol
(D) Topical clindamycin
(E) Doxycycline
gpt-4-32k Response: (A) Itraconazole

This patient's clinical presentation is suggestive of tinea corporis (ringworm), a superficial fungal infection of the skin. The rash is typically annular with a raised, erythematous, scaly border and central clearing, and it may be pruritic. It is caused by dermatophytes, which are fungi that invade the stratum corneum, nails, or hair. Risk factors include exposure to infected humans or animals and immunosuppression. The diagnosis is confirmed by KOH preparation of skin scrapings, which shows hyphae. Treatment is with topical antifungal agents (eg, clotrimazole, terbinafine) for localized disease. Oral antifungal agents (eg, itraconazole, terbinafine) are used for extensive disease, tinea in hair-bearing areas, or tinea unresponsive to topical treatment.

Question: An unconscious 55-year-old man is brought to the Emergency Department by ambulance. He had recently lost his job and his house was about to begin foreclosure. There were several empty bottles of vodka around him and one half empty container of antifreeze. A review of his medical records reveals that he was previously in good health. Upon arrival to the ED he regains consciousness.  His blood pressure is 135/85 mmHg, heart rate 120/min, respiratory rate 22/min, and temperature 36.5°C (97.7°F). On physical exam his speech is slurred and he has difficulty following commands. His abdomen is diffusely tender to palpation with no rebound tenderness. Initial laboratory tests show an elevated serum creatinine (Cr) of 1.9 mg/dL, and blood urea nitrogen (BUN) of 29 mg/dL. Which of the following findings would be expected in this patient’s urine?(A) Eosinophilia
(B) Calcium oxalate crystals
(C) Hyaline casts
(D) Urine ketones
(E) Urate crystals
gpt-4-32k Response: (B) Calcium oxalate crystals

This patient's history of heavy alcohol use, recent stressors, and the finding of an antifreeze container suggest a suicide attempt by ethylene glycol (antifreeze) ingestion. Ethylene glycol is metabolized in the liver to toxic metabolites, causing an elevated anion gap metabolic acidosis. Initial symptoms may include inebriation, vomiting, and abdominal pain. As the toxic metabolites accumulate, patients may develop tachypnea, tachycardia, and altered mental status. The metabolites can precipitate in the kidneys, causing acute tubular necrosis and renal failure (as suggested by this patient's elevated Cr and BUN). Oxalate, one of the metabolites, can precipitate with calcium in the urine to form envelope-shaped calcium oxalate crystals.

Question: A 55-year-old woman with a history of HIV presents to the emergency department with progressive dyspnea, fever, and dry cough for the past 4 days. She has not been compliant with antiretroviral or prophylactic medication. On evaluation, the patient is in moderate respiratory distress. The temperature is 38.8°C (102.0°F), the blood pressure is 124/82 mm Hg, the pulse is 96/min, and the respiratory rate is 20/min. Pulse oximetry is 92% on 4 L oxygen by nasal cannula. Her CD4 count is 180 cells/μL. IV antibiotics and glucocorticoids are administered. After 30 minutes, the patient develops severe respiratory distress. Repeat vital signs show: temperature 38.3°C (101.0°F), blood pressure 80/50 mm Hg, pulse 104/min, respiration rate 32/min and pulse oximetry 85% on nasal cannula. The trachea deviates to the left. Breath sounds are absent on the right side and the neck veins are distended. Telemetry shows sinus tachycardia. Which of the following is the best next step in the management of this patient?(A) Discontinue antibiotics
(B) Administer epinephrine
(C) Perform an arterial blood gas
(D) Needle thoracostomy
(E) Stat chest X-ray
gpt-4-32k Response: (D) Needle thoracostomy

This patient's acute onset of hypotension, hypoxia, tachycardia, and distended neck veins, in conjunction with tracheal deviation and decreased breath sounds on one side, is suggestive of a tension pneumothorax. This is a life-threatening condition that occurs when air enters the pleural space during inspiration but cannot exit during expiration, leading to a buildup of pressure that compresses the lung and shifts the mediastinum. This can impede venous return to the heart, leading to decreased cardiac output and shock. Tension pneumothorax can be triggered by barotrauma from mechanical ventilation, but it can also occur spontaneously, particularly in patients with underlying lung disease. This patient's HIV and noncompliance with antiretroviral therapy put her at risk for opportunistic lung infections, which can cause necrosis and rupture of alveoli. The immediate treatment for tension pneumothorax is needle decompression (needle thoracostomy), followed by chest tube placement. This should be done as soon as the diagnosis is suspected, without waiting for imaging confirmation, as the condition can rapidly lead to cardiovascular collapse.
--------------------------------------------------------------------------
[PROMPT HIDDEN FOR CLARITY]
--------Reason--------
Output is correct. The model has correctly identified the symptoms and the condition the patient is experiencing, which is tension pneumothorax, and has suggested the appropriate next step in management, which is needle thoracostomy. The explanation provided by the model is detailed and accurate, explaining the reasons behind the diagnosis and the urgency of the treatment.

Her vital signs are as follows: temperature - 38.8°C (102.0°F), blood pressure - 124/82 mm Hg, pulse - 96/min, respiratory rate - 20/min. Her oxygen saturation is 92% while on 4 L oxygen through a nasal cannula. Her CD4 count is 180 cells/μL. (B) Administer epinephrine

This can impede venous return to the heart, leading to decreased cardiac output and shock. There are no changes in the skin or nipple. There is no palpable cervical or axillary adenopathy. Mammography shows a smooth polylobulated mass. Biopsy of the mass shows papillary projections of epithelial-lined stroma with hyperplasia and atypia. Which of the following is the most likely diagnosis?(A) Intraductal papilloma
(B) Phyllodes tumor
(C) Fibroadenoma
(D) Fibrocystic disease of the breast
(E) Invasive lobular carcinoma
gpt-4-32k Response: (B) Phyllodes tumor
--------------------------------------------------------------------------
[PROMPT HIDDEN FOR CLARITY]
--------Reason--------
correct. The problem statement is clear and provides sufficient information to identify the correct answer. Phyllodes tumors are characterized by a rapidly growing mass, which aligns with the patient's description. They are also known to be multinodular and large, often larger than 3 cm, which fits with the physical examination findings. The histology of these tumors typically shows a leaf-like pattern with stromal overgrowth, which matches the biopsy results. Therefore, the correct diagnosis for this patient is a Phyllodes tumor.

Question: A 32-year-old woman comes to the physician because of a 3-week history of intermittent loose stools and a 1.2-kg (2.6-lb) weight loss. She immigrated to the US from Uganda 6 weeks ago. Abdominal examination shows diffuse tenderness with no guarding or rebound. The liver is firm and palpable 3 cm below the right costal margin, and the spleen is palpable just below the left costal margin. Her leukocyte count is 12,800/mm3 (12% eosinophils). Stool culture shows several oval-shaped eggs with lateral spines. Microscopic examination of a liver biopsy specimen shows granulomatous inflammation with periportal fibrosis. Exposure to which of the following is most likely to have played a role in the development of this patient's symptoms?(A) Tsetse flies
(B) Undercooked pork meat
(C) Undercooked fish meat
(D) Dog feces
(E) Freshwater snails
gpt-4-32k Response: (E) Freshwater snails

This patient's presentation is suggestive of Schistosomiasis, specifically Schistosoma mansoni, which is endemic in Africa and associated with freshwater snail exposure. The eggs of S. mansoni are oval-shaped with a lateral spine. Symptoms can include abdominal pain and diarrhea. Chronic infection can lead to liver fibrosis and portal hypertension, as suggested by the patient's hepatosplenomegaly and periportal fibrosis on liver biopsy. Eosinophilia is also commonly seen in parasitic infections, including schistosomiasis.

1. Monotheists: These are individuals who believe in the existence of one God. Examples include Christians, Muslims, and Jews.

2. Polytheists: These are individuals who believe in the existence of multiple gods. Examples include Hindus and ancient Greeks and Romans.

3. Pantheists: These are individuals who believe that God is not a personal deity but is identical with the universe and nature.

4. Deists: These are individuals who believe in the existence of a creator who does not intervene in the universe. The creator can be a personal or impersonal deity.

5. Henotheists: These are individuals who believe in the existence of multiple gods but worship only one of them.

6. Dualists: These are individuals who believe in the existence of two equally powerful gods, often representing good and evil.

7. Monotheistic Polytheists: These are individuals who believe in the existence of multiple gods but consider one of them as supreme.

8. Pandeists: These are individuals who believe that God created the universe and then became the universe.

9. Panentheists: These are individuals who believe that God pervades and interpenetrates every part of the universe and also extends beyond time and space.

10. Theistic agnostics: These are individuals who are uncertain about the existence of God but choose to believe in God. 

These are not exhaustive, as there are many different ways that people can believe in a higher power or powers.

8. Pandeists: These are individuals who believe that God created the universe and then became the universe.

9. Panentheists: These are individuals who believe that God pervades and interpenetrates every part of the universe and also extends beyond time and space.

10. Theistic agnostics: These are individuals who are uncertain about the existence of God but choose to believe in God. 

These are not exhaustive, as there are many different ways that people can believe in a higher power or powers.

Freshwater snails

Question: An 18-month-old girl is brought to the emergency department because of a cough that her parents are worried about. She has had a runny nose and a low-grade fever for the past 2 days, with some hoarseness and a rough-sounding cough that started this afternoon. This evening she began making some high-pitched sounds when taking breaths, and she seemed to be having some trouble breathing. She is alert and does not appear to be in acute distress. She has a temperature of 38.0°C (100.4 °F), with a respiratory rate of 50/min and O2 saturation of 97%. There is audible inspiratory stridor that worsens when she starts to cry during the examination. She has an occasional barking cough. Her pharynx is mildly erythematous with normal tonsils and no exudate. A frontal X-ray of the upper chest airways is obtained (shown in the image). Which of the following is the best step in management?(A) Anterior-posterior and lateral radiographs of the neck
(B) Intubation
(C) Racemic epinephrine and intramuscular corticosteroid therapy
(D) Intravenous antibiotics
(E) Trial of bronchodilator therapy and oral steroids
gpt-4-32k Response: This child's symptoms of low-grade fever, hoarseness, barking cough, and inspiratory stridor are characteristic of croup (laryngotracheobronchitis), the most common cause of upper airway obstruction in children 6 months to 6 years of age. Croup is most often caused by parainfluenza virus. The subglottic area of the trachea is the narrowest part of the airway in children and is the site most affected by the subglottic edema that occurs in croup. This leads to the characteristic symptoms of croup, including a barking (seal-like) cough and varying degrees of respiratory distress, ranging from tachypnea and mild retractions to severe respiratory distress with stridor at rest. A chest x-ray is usually not needed for diagnosis but, if obtained, may show a characteristic "steeple sign" caused by subglottic narrowing.
The management of croup is based on the severity of symptoms. Mild croup can be managed with supportive care, including mist therapy with humidified air. Moderate to severe croup, characterized by stridor at rest, is managed with a single dose of dexamethasone and nebulized epinephrine (Choice C). Dexamethasone reduces airway edema, and nebulized epinephrine causes alpha-agonist-mediated vasoconstriction, which reduces airway edema and improves symptoms. However, the effect of nebulized epinephrine is temporary, and patients must be observed for a few hours after administration to monitor for recurrence of symptoms.

Question: A 15-year-old boy is brought to the emergency department because of severe abdominal pain and vomiting for 8 hours. He has had around 3–4 episodes of greenish colored vomit during this period. He has major depressive disorder with psychosis. His mother has Graves' disease. Current medications include sertraline and haloperidol. He appears uncomfortable. His temperature is 37.3°C (99.1°F), pulse is 87/min, and blood pressure is 118/72 mm Hg. He is diagnosed with acute appendicitis and is taken to the operating room for an emergency laparoscopic appendectomy. The appendix is gangrenous and perforated with purulent fluid in the pelvis. Just after the appendiceal base is ligated, the patient shows muscle rigidity and profuse diaphoresis. His temperature is 39.1°C (102.4°F), pulse is 130/min, and blood pressure is 146/70 mm Hg. The pupils are equal and reactive to light. The end-tidal CO2 is 85 mm Hg. Which of the following is the most appropriate treatment for this patient's condition?(A) Propranolol
(B) Dantrolene
(C) Bromocriptine
(D) Cyproheptadine
(E) Surgical embolectomy
gpt-4-32k Response: (B) Dantrolene

The patient's presentation of muscle rigidity, hyperthermia, tachycardia, hypertension, and elevated end-tidal CO2 after receiving haloperidol (a dopamine receptor antagonist) and undergoing surgery is suggestive of malignant hyperthermia (MH). MH is a potentially fatal, inherited disorder usually triggered by exposure to certain medications used for general anesthesia (eg, succinylcholine, volatile anesthetics). The underlying defect is in the ryanodine receptor, causing an uncontrolled release of calcium from the sarcoplasmic reticulum in skeletal muscle and resulting in sustained muscle contraction. Clinical features include hyperthermia, muscle rigidity (especially masseter muscle, also known as trismus or "jaws of steel"), and signs of increased metabolic activity (eg, tachycardia, tachypnea, acidosis). Diagnosis is clinical, and management involves immediate discontinuation of the triggering agent, cooling measures, and administration of dantrolene. Dantrolene acts by inhibiting calcium release from the sarcoplasmic reticulum of skeletal muscle, thereby reducing muscle contractions.
--------------------------------------------------------------------------
[PROMPT HIDDEN FOR CLARITY]
--------Reason--------
Output is correct. The AI has correctly identified the patient's condition as malignant hyperthermia and correctly selected Dantrolene as the most appropriate treatment. The reasoning provided is accurate and aligns with medical knowledge. The AI also correctly interpreted the prompt and followed the instruction to choose the most appropriate treatment from the given options.

Question: A 6-year-old boy is brought to the physician by his mother because of a 6-month history of mild episodic abdominal pain. The episodes occur every 1–2 months and last for a few hours. The pain is located in the epigastrium, radiates to his back, and is occasionally associated with mild nausea. His mother is concerned that his condition might be hereditary because his older sister was diagnosed with congenital heart disease. He is otherwise healthy and has met all developmental milestones. He is at the 75th percentile for height and the 65th percentile for weight. Physical examination shows no abdominal distention, guarding, or rebound tenderness. Which of the following congenital conditions would best explain this patient's symptoms?(A) Hypertrophic pyloric stenosis
(B) Biliary cyst
(C) Tracheoesophageal fistula
(D) Intestinal malrotation
(E) Pancreas divisum
gpt-4-32k Response: (E) Pancreas divisum

The patient's episodic epigastric abdominal pain radiating to the back is characteristic of pancreatitis. Pancreas divisum is the most common congenital pancreatic anomaly, occurring when the dorsal and ventral pancreatic buds fail to fuse during the seventh week of gestation. This condition results in the majority of the pancreas (ie, the dorsal bud) draining through the minor papilla, which has a smaller diameter than the major papilla. Most patients with pancreas divisum are asymptomatic; however, in a small number of patients, the minor papilla provides insufficient drainage, leading to recurrent bouts of pancreatitis.

Question: A 30-year-old woman comes to the physician because she has been unable to conceive for 3 years. Analysis of her husband's semen has shown normal sperm counts during this time. The patient also reports episodic pelvic and back pain accompanied by painful diarrhea for 5 years. She has about one such episode on average per month for 4–6 days. She has taken ibuprofen for the pain, which has provided some relief. Menses have occurred at regular 29-day intervals since menarche at the age of 14 years and last for 7 days. She is sexually active with her husband and does not use contraception. Vital signs are within normal limits. Pelvic and bimanual examinations are normal; rectal examination is unremarkable. A hysterosalpingogram 6 months prior showed normal results. Which of the following is the most likely underlying mechanism of this patient's symptoms?(A) Loss of fallopian tube function following infection
(B) Smooth muscle tumor arising from the myometrium
(C) Increased secretion of prolactin
(D) Endometrial tissue outside the uterine cavity
(E) Increased secretion of androgens and luteinizing hormone
gpt-4-32k Response: (D) Endometrial tissue outside the uterine cavity

This patient's chronic pelvic pain, dysmenorrhea, and infertility are suggestive of endometriosis, a condition characterized by the presence of endometrial glands and stroma outside the uterine cavity. The most commonly involved sites are the ovaries, uterosacral ligaments, and the pouch of Douglas. The ectopic endometrial tissue responds to hormonal stimulation in a manner similar to the normal endometrium, leading to cyclic bleeding. This can cause pain and inflammation, and over time, can result in the formation of adhesions and fibrosis. The pain associated with endometriosis is typically most severe just before and during menses and may be associated with deep dyspareunia and bowel or bladder symptoms. Although endometriosis is a common cause of infertility, the exact mechanism is unclear. Proposed mechanisms include anatomic distortion from adhesions, altered peritoneal function, impaired implantation, and altered hormonal and immune environment within the peritoneal cavity. The diagnosis of endometriosis can be suggested by clinical findings but is definitively made by direct visualization of endometrial implants during laparoscopy.

Question: A routine newborn screening test for phenylketonuria in a male neonate shows a serum phenylalanine concentration of 44 mg/dL (N < 20). He is started on a special diet and the hyperphenylalaninemia resolves. At a routine well-child examination 4 months later, the physician notices that he has persistent head lag. On examination, he has blue eyes, pale skin, blonde hair, and generalized hypotonia. His serum prolactin level is markedly elevated. Supplementation of which of the following substances is most likely to prevent further complications of this patient's condition?(A) Tyrosine
(B) Pyridoxine
(C) Thiamine
(D) Tetrahydrobiopterin
(E) Niacin
gpt-4-32k Response: (A) Tyrosine

This child's symptoms are suggestive of phenylketonuria (PKU), an autosomal recessive disorder caused by a deficiency in phenylalanine hydroxylase or its cofactor tetrahydrobiopterin (BH4). Phenylalanine hydroxylase is needed to convert phenylalanine to tyrosine. If this enzyme is deficient, phenylalanine accumulates and is converted to phenylketones, which are excreted in the urine. 

The initial presentation includes a "musty" or "mousy" odor of the urine and sweat, intellectual disability, seizures, fair skin, and eczema. The fair skin, blonde hair, and blue eyes are due to decreased production of melanin because tyrosine is a precursor for melanin synthesis. 

Treatment involves a diet low in phenylalanine and supplemented with tyrosine. In the absence of treatment, most individuals with PKU will develop profound intellectual disability. With early and continuous treatment, however, affected individuals can have normal development and lifespan. 

BH4 is also a cofactor for the synthesis of dopamine, and deficiency can lead to decreased synthesis of dopamine and elevated prolactin levels (due to lack of dopamine inhibition on prolactin release). However, supplementation with BH4 is not typically part of the treatment for PKU because the primary problem is a deficiency of phenylalanine hydroxylase, not BH4.
--------------------------------------------------------------------------
[PROMPT HIDDEN FOR CLARITY]
--------Reason--------
Output is correct. The AI has correctly identified the symptoms and the condition (phenylketonuria) and suggested the correct treatment (Tyrosine). The AI has also provided a detailed explanation of the condition and the reason for the chosen treatment.

(A) Tyrosine
(B) Pyridoxine
(C) Thiamine
(D) Tetrahydrobiopterin
(E) Niacin
Please provide your answer along with a brief explanation of the reasoning behind your choice.

This child's symptoms are suggestive of phenylketonuria (PKU), an autosomal recessive disorder caused by a deficiency in phenylalanine hydroxylase or its cofactor tetrahydrobiopterin (BH4). Phenylalanine hydroxylase is needed to convert phenylalanine to tyrosine. If this enzyme is deficient, phenylalanine accumulates and is converted to phenylketones, which are excreted in the urine. 

The initial presentation includes a "musty" or "mousy" odor of the urine and sweat, intellectual disability, seizures, fair skin, and eczema. The fair skin, blonde hair, and blue eyes are due to decreased production of melanin because tyrosine is a precursor for melanin synthesis. 

Treatment involves a diet low in phenylalanine and supplemented with tyrosine. In the absence of treatment, most individuals with PKU will develop profound intellectual disability. With early and continuous treatment, however, affected individuals can have normal development and lifespan. 

BH4 is also a cofactor for the synthesis of dopamine, and deficiency can lead to decreased synthesis of dopamine and elevated prolactin levels (due to lack of dopamine inhibition on prolactin release). Question: A 3-year-old boy is brought to the physician for a well-child examination. Over the past 8 months, his mother reports difficulty understanding the boy's speech. On occasion during this period, she has noticed that he does not respond when called by name and cannot follow 1-step instructions. He has a history of recurrent ear infections treated with antibiotics since birth. He is at the 60th percentile for length and 50th percentile for weight. Vital signs are within normal limits. His speech is quiet and difficult to understand. Otoscopic examination shows retracted tympanic membranes bilaterally that are immobile on pneumatic otoscopy. Nasopharyngoscopy shows mild adenoid hypertrophy. Pure tone audiometry shows a conductive hearing loss of 26 dB on the right side and 28 dB on the left side. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?(A) Nasal decongestant therapy
(B) Adenoidectomy
(C) Tympanostomy tube insertion
(D) Antihistamine therapy
(E) Corticosteroid therapy
"
gpt-4-32k Response: (C) Tympanostomy tube insertion

This child's history of recurrent otitis media, difficulty understanding his speech, and not responding when called by name suggest chronic serous otitis media (CSOM), also known as otitis media with effusion. CSOM is characterized by the presence of middle ear effusion behind an intact tympanic membrane for more than 3 months without signs or symptoms of acute infection. The effusion results from the impaired ventilation of the middle ear due to eustachian tube dysfunction, which can be caused by anatomic factors (eg, cleft palate), environmental factors (eg, tobacco smoke), or adenoid hypertrophy/infection. Patients may be asymptomatic or present with hearing loss or speech delay. On examination, the tympanic membrane appears dull, retracted, and immobile on pneumatic otoscopy.

Management is initially conservative, as many cases resolve spontaneously. However, if the effusion persists for more than 3 months, the child has hearing loss (>20 dB), or there are symptoms of speech delay, learning problems, or behavioral issues, tympanostomy tube insertion is indicated. The tubes help drain the effusion and ventilate the middle ear, thereby improving hearing and speech. Although this child has mild adenoid hypertrophy, adenoidectomy is typically reserved for children who fail tympanostomy tube placement or have significant nasal obstruction or chronic sinusitis.

Question: A 37-year-old woman presents to the clinic with pain and swelling of her legs. She states that she first noticed the swelling 6 weeks ago. Initially, the swelling was minimal, but now she is finding it difficult to walk and she can’t fit into any of her shoes. Her medical history is significant for systemic lupus erythematosus. She takes ibuprofen and hydroxychloroquine. Her temperature is 99°F (37.2°C), blood pressure is 153/86 mmHg, and pulse is 88 beats/min. Pitting edema to bilateral knees is noted on physical examination. Urinalysis reveals proteinuria. The patient is started on an immunosuppressant. Two days later the patient returns to clinic complaining of blood in her urine. A urinalysis reveals moderate erythrocytes but is negative for leukocyte esterase, nitrites, or crystals. Diffuse oozing of the bladder mucosa with neovascularization is seen on cystoscopy. Which of the following could have prevented the patient’s condition?(A) Ifosfamide
(B) Mesna
(C) Methotrexate
(D) Nitrofurantoin
(E) Prednisone
gpt-4-32k Response: (B) Mesna
--------------------------------------------------------------------------
[PROMPT HIDDEN FOR CLARITY]
--------Reason--------
The output is correct. The patient's presentation of proteinuria and hematuria following the initiation of an immunosuppressant is suggestive of drug-induced hemorrhagic cystitis. Hemorrhagic cystitis is a known complication of cyclophosphamide and ifosfamide. Mesna is a uroprotective agent that binds to acrolein, a metabolite of cyclophosphamide and ifosfamide, and prevents bladder toxicity. Therefore, the use of mesna could have prevented the patient's condition.

Question: Please refer to the summary above to answer this question
A cardiologist at another institution is trying to replicate the table in the abstract with a very similar study that he is conducting. After collecting data on changes in plaque volume, LDL-C levels, FEV1/FVC ratio, and ACQ scores from baseline to 12-month follow-up, he is curious if the changes in the aforementioned measurements are statistically significant. Which of the following is the most appropriate statistical method to compare the groups?"
"Impact of pulmharkimab on asthma control and cardiovascular disease progression in patients with coronary artery disease and comorbid asthma
Introduction:
Active asthma has been found to be associated with a more than two-fold increase in the risk of myocardial infarction, even after adjusting for cardiovascular risk factors. It has been suggested that the inflammatory mediators and accelerated atherosclerosis characterizing systemic inflammation may increase the risk of both asthma and cardiovascular disease. This study evaluated the efficacy of the novel IL-1 inhibitor pulmharkimab in improving asthma and cardiovascular disease progression.
Methods:
In this double-blind, randomized controlled trial, patients (N=1200) with a history of coronary artery disease, myocardial infarction in the past 2 years, and a diagnosis of comorbid adult-onset asthma were recruited from cardiology clinics at a large academic medical center in Philadelphia, PA. Patients who were immunocompromised or had a history of recurrent infections were excluded.
Patients were subsequently randomly assigned a 12-month course of pulmharkimab 75 mg/day, pulmharkimab 150 mg/day, or a placebo, with each group containing 400 participants. All participants were included in analysis and analyzed in the groups to which they were randomized regardless of medication adherence. Variables measured included plaque volume, serum LDL-C levels, FEV1/FVC ratio, and Asthma Control Questionnaire (ACQ) scores, which quantified the severity of asthma symptoms. Plaque volume was determined by ultrasound.
Analyses were performed from baseline to month 12.
Results:
At baseline, participants in the two groups did not differ by age, gender, race, plaque volume, serum LDL-C levels, FEV1/FVC ratio, and ACQ scores (p > 0.05 for all). A total of 215 participants (18%) were lost to follow-up. At 12-month follow-up, the groups contained the following numbers of participants:
Pulmharkimab 75 mg/d: 388 participants
Pulmharkimab 150 mg/d: 202 participants
Placebo: 395 participants
Table 1: Association between pulmharkimab and both pulmonary and cardiovascular outcomes. Models were adjusted for sociodemographic variables and medical comorbidities. All outcome variables were approximately normally distributed.
Pulmharkimab 75 mg/d, (Mean +/- 2 SE) Pulmharkimab 150 mg/d, (Mean +/- 2 SE) Placebo, (Mean +/- 2 SE) P-value
Plaque volume (mm3), change from baseline 6.6 ± 2.8 1.2 ± 4.7 15.8 ± 2.9 < 0.01
LDL-C levels, change from baseline -9.4 ± 3.6 -11.2 ± 14.3 -8.4 ± 3.9 0.28
FEV1/FVC ratio, change from baseline 0.29 ± 2.21 0.34 ± 5.54 -0.22 ± 3.21 0.27
ACQ scores, change from baseline 0.31 ± 1.22 0.46 ± 3.25 0.12 ± 1.33 0.43
Conclusion:
Pulmharkimab may be effective in reducing plaque volume but does not lead to improved asthma control in patients with a history of myocardial infarction and comorbid asthma.
Source of funding: Southeast Institute for Advanced Lung and Cardiovascular Studies, American Center for Advancement of Cardiovascular Health"(A) Pearson correlation analysis
(B) Chi-square test

(B) Impaired attention is a key feature of the disorder
(C) Intellectual disability is a key feature of the disorder
(D) It is usually associated with a family history of schizophrenia

[PROMPT HIDDEN FOR CLARITY]
--------Reason--------
correct. The patient's difficulty in understanding social cues, intense interest in a specific topic, and resistance to change are suggestive of autism spectrum disorder (ASD). ASD is more common in boys than in girls. Other answer choices are incorrect because they do not accurately represent the features of ASD. For example, while attention problems can occur in individuals with ASD, they are not a key feature of the disorder. Similarly, while some individuals with ASD may have intellectual disability, it is not a key feature of the disorder. There is no established link between ASD and a family history of schizophrenia. Lastly, the typical age of onset of ASD is not 3-5 years of age; symptoms often appear by age 2.

Question: A 3-year-old boy is brought to his pediatrician for evaluation because his parents have been concerned by a number of troubling signs. Specifically, they have noticed that he has been exhibiting aggressive behavior as well as hyperactivity. In addition, he has had intellectual disability and is slow to hit developmental milestones. Physical exam reveals coarse facies, short stature, and joint stiffness. Eye exam reveals no abnormalities of the cornea or lens. Based on these findings, the patient is referred to a geneticist for further evaluation. Which of the following substances would most likely accumulate in the cells of this patient?(A) Cerebroside sulfate
(B) Galactocerebroside
(C) Glucocerebroside
(D) Heparan sulfate
(E) Sphingomyelin
gpt-4-32k Response: (D) Heparan sulfate

This patient's coarse facies, short stature, joint stiffness, intellectual disability, and behavioral problems suggest a diagnosis of Hurler syndrome (mucopolysaccharidosis type I). Hurler syndrome is an autosomal recessive disorder caused by a deficiency of α-L-iduronidase, which results in accumulation of heparan sulfate and dermatan sulfate in the lysosomes of many tissues. This accumulation leads to a variety of symptoms, including developmental delay, coarse facial features, corneal clouding, hepatosplenomegaly, and skeletal abnormalities (dysostosis multiplex).

Question: An 18-year-old college student presents to the student health clinic complaining of excessive sleepiness. He first noticed the daytime sleepiness about a year ago but reports that it significantly worsened once he started college. He reports that he goes to bed around 9 p.m. and wakes up for class at 8 a.m.. He naps between classes for a total of 4 hours during the day. Despite this, he wakes up feeling exhausted. He easily falls asleep during class and while studying. This has started to affect both his academics and his social life. On weekends, he can sleep up to 20 hours a day. He reports 2 recent episodes of sudden generalized body weakness along with ptosis and difficulty speaking that occurred after he became angry. Each episode lasted about 10 seconds, but he says he felt back to normal within 15 minutes without any persistent deficits. His Epworth Sleepiness Scale score is 17. Which of the following is the best next step in therapy?(A) Clomipramine
(B) Dextroamphetamine
(C) Methylpenidate
(D) Modafinil
(E) Sleep deprivation
gpt-4-32k Response: (D) Modafinil

This patient's excessive daytime sleepiness, fragmented sleep, and episodes of cataplexy (eg, sudden loss of muscle tone triggered by strong emotions) are suggestive of narcolepsy. Other common symptoms include sleep paralysis and hypnagogic/hypnopompic hallucinations. Patients with narcolepsy have a deficiency in hypocretin (orexin), a neurotransmitter that promotes wakefulness and inhibits rapid eye movement (REM) sleep. As a result, patients have intrusions of REM sleep phenomena into wakefulness (eg, cataplexy, hypnagogic hallucinations) and difficulty maintaining wakefulness during the day.

First-line treatment for narcolepsy includes scheduled naps and wake-promoting agents such as modafinil. Modafinil is a non-amphetamine stimulant that works through an unknown mechanism to promote wakefulness. It is preferred over amphetamine stimulants (eg, dextroamphetamine, methylphenidate) because it has less risk of tolerance, addiction, and adverse cardiovascular effects.

The selective serotonin reuptake inhibitor and tricyclic antidepressants (eg, clomipramine) are second-line treatment options for cataplexy if it persists despite treatment with modafinil. They are thought to suppress REM sleep, thereby reducing cataplexy. However, they do not address the excessive daytime sleepiness, which is this patient's most problematic symptom.
--------------------------------------------------------------------------

Question: Six days after undergoing an aortic valve replacement surgery, a 68-year-old woman has bluish discolorations around the peripheral intravenous catheters and severe right leg pain. She has a history of hypertension and major depressive disorder. She drinks 3 beers daily. Current medications include hydrochlorothiazide, aspirin, prophylactic unfractionated heparin, and fluoxetine. Her temperature is 37.3°C (99.1°F), pulse is 90/min, and blood pressure is 118/92 mm Hg. Examination shows multiple hematomas on the upper extremities around the peripheral intravenous access sites. There is a healing sternal incision. The lungs are clear to auscultation. Cardiac examination shows an aortic click. The right calf is tender, erythematous and swollen. Laboratory studies show:
Hemoglobin 13.1 g/dL
Leukocyte count 9,900/mm3
Platelet count 48,000/mm3
Prothrombin time 15 seconds
Activated partial thromboplastin time 40 seconds
Serum
Urea nitrogen 19 mg/dL
Creatinine 1.1 mg/dL
Alkaline phosphatase 33 U/L
AST 26 U/L
ALT 33 U/L
γ-Glutamyl transferase 45 U/L (N=5–50 U/L)
Which of the following is the most likely underlying mechanism of this patient's symptoms?"(A) Deficiency of vitamin K-dependent clotting factors
(B) Phagocytosis of autoantibody-opsonized platelets
(C) Deficiency of von Willebrand factor
(D) Excess circulating thrombin
(E) Autoantibody-mediated platelet activation
gpt-4-32k Response: This patient's presentation of thrombocytopenia, thrombosis (eg, deep vein thrombosis), and bleeding (eg, hematoma) after undergoing surgery and receiving unfractionated heparin suggests heparin-induced thrombocytopenia (HIT). HIT is a prothrombotic disorder caused by autoantibody-mediated platelet activation (choice E). The autoantibodies recognize complexes of platelet factor 4 (PF4) and heparin, leading to platelet activation. Activated platelets release more PF4, which propagates the cycle. The resulting thrombocytopenia is usually mild to moderate (platelet count 60,000-150,000/mm3), and severe thrombocytopenia (<20,000/mm3) is rare.
The thrombosis in HIT is due to excessive thrombin generation. It can cause venous or arterial thrombosis, and venous thrombosis is more common. The most common sites of venous thrombosis are the deep veins of the leg and the pulmonary arteries. Less common sites include the veins of the arm and the cerebral sinuses. Arterial thrombosis can occur in any artery but most commonly affects the cerebral and coronary arteries.
HIT typically occurs 5-10 days after heparin exposure. The diagnosis is confirmed by detecting antibodies against the heparin-PF4 complex. Treatment involves discontinuation of all forms of heparin and initiation of a direct thrombin inhibitor (eg, argatroban, bivalirudin) or a factor Xa inhibitor (eg, fondaparinux).

Each semester usually lasts around 15 weeks, although this can vary slightly depending on the university.

The fall semester typically begins in late August or early September and runs until mid-December. There is then a winter break, which lasts until the start of the spring semester in January. The spring semester usually ends in early to mid-May, followed by a summer break.

In addition to the fall and spring semesters, many universities also offer optional summer sessions. These are shorter terms that typically run from late May or early June until July or August. These sessions allow students to take additional courses and potentially graduate earlier.

However, not all universities follow this traditional semester system. Some operate on a quarter system, which divides the academic year into four terms: fall, winter, spring, and summer. Each quarter is typically 10 weeks long. Other universities may use a trimester system, with three terms per year, or a block plan, where students take one course at a time for a few weeks. 

It's also important to note that the start and end dates of semesters can vary widely between universities, so it's always best to check the specific academic calendar for the institution in question.

The Hubble provides high-resolution images of distant galaxies and nebulae, and its data is used to measure the rate of expansion of the universe, among other things. Unlike ground-based telescopes, Hubble can observe high-frequency ultraviolet light that is normally absorbed by the Earth's atmosphere.

--------True Answer--------
Echocardiogram

Question: A 70-year-old man is brought to the emergency department by his wife for incomprehensible speech. Upon first inspection you note the man is drooling and his eyes are tearing excessively. The patient's wife explains that the man was tilling the fields on the family beet farm when she found him collapsed on a pile of freshly picked beets. The patient seemed confused so the wife brought him in promptly, and states it has only been ten minutes since the patient was found down in the fields. Physical exam is deferred due to a confused and combative patient who is speaking clearly yet his sentences do not make any sense. The patients clothes are removed in the ED. Vital signs are currently being obtained. Which of the following is the best initial step in management?(A) Sublingual atropine drops
(B) Neostigmine
(C) Physostigmine
(D) 2-pralidoxime
(E) Intubation
gpt-4-32k Response: This patient's symptoms of altered mental status, excessive salivation, lacrimation, and potentially other symptoms not yet identified such as urinary incontinence, defecation, and emesis are consistent with organophosphate poisoning. Organophosphates are found in many insecticides and nerve gases and cause symptoms by inhibiting acetylcholinesterase, leading to an excess of acetylcholine. The excess acetylcholine then overstimulates muscarinic and nicotinic receptors, leading to the symptoms seen in this patient. Treatment of organophosphate poisoning involves decontamination, supportive care, and specific antidotes. Decontamination is the most important initial step and includes removing the patient's clothes and washing the skin. Supportive care includes ensuring a patent airway, adequate breathing, and circulation. The antidotes for organophosphate poisoning are atropine and pralidoxime. Atropine is an antimuscarinic agent that can help with the muscarinic symptoms of organophosphate poisoning such as bradycardia, miosis, bronchorrhea, and bronchospasm. Pralidoxime regenerates acetylcholinesterase if given early enough and can help with both muscarinic and nicotinic symptoms. However, before these antidotes can be given, the patient must be decontaminated to prevent further exposure to the organophosphate. Therefore, the best initial step in management is decontamination by removing the patient's clothes and washing the skin (choice E).
--------------------------------------------------------------------------
[PROMPT HIDDEN FOR CLARITY]

Question: A 45-year-old Caucasian male presents to a gastroenterologist complaining of heartburn and difficulty swallowing. He recalls that he has been told by his primary care physician that he suffers from gastroesophageal reflux disease (GERD). The gastroenterologist decides to perform an upper endoscopy with biopsy. Which of the following findings would be consistent with Barrett's esophagus?(A) Presence of Paneth cells in the lower esophagus
(B) Metaplasia in the upper esophagus
(C) A small region of red, velvet-like mucosa in the lower esophagus
(D) Esophageal varices
(E) Neutrophilic infiltration
gpt-4-32k Response: (C) A small region of red, velvet-like mucosa in the lower esophagus
--------------------------------------------------------------------------
[PROMPT HIDDEN FOR CLARITY]
--------Reason--------
correct. Barrett's esophagus is a condition in which the normal squamous epithelium lining the lower esophagus is replaced by columnar epithelium, usually due to chronic GERD. This change is a form of metaplasia, which is a reversible change from one cell type to another in response to chronic irritation or inflammation. The columnar epithelium appears as a red, velvet-like mucosa during endoscopy, which is different from the normal pale pink color of the squamous epithelium. Therefore, the correct answer is (C) A small region of red, velvet-like mucosa in the lower esophagus.

Question: A 75-year-old man comes to the physician for the evaluation of progressive shortness of breath and fatigue over the past month. He reports that he cannot climb more than one flight of stairs without experiencing shortness of breath and dizziness. He has hypertension and hyperlipidemia. He has smoked one pack of cigarettes daily for the past 50 years. He does not drink alcohol. His medications include enalapril, atorvastatin, and low-dose aspirin. His temperature is 37°C (98.6°F), pulse is 70/min, respirations are 18/min, and blood pressure is 100/80 mm Hg. Physical examination shows weak peripheral pulses. Cardiac examination is shown. Which of the following is the most likely diagnosis?(A) Aortic valve regurgitation
(B) Tricuspid valve stenosis
(C) Mitral valve prolapse
(D) Mitral valve stenosis
(E) Aortic valve stenosis
gpt-4-32k Response: The patient's symptoms of progressive exertional dyspnea and dizziness, along with a physical examination showing weak peripheral pulses and a systolic ejection murmur, are suggestive of aortic stenosis (AS). AS is most commonly caused by age-related calcific degeneration in developed countries. Risk factors for calcific AS include older age, male sex, hypertension, smoking, hyperlipidemia, and a history of rheumatic heart disease. The murmur of AS is a crescendo-decrescendo systolic ejection murmur best heard at the right second intercostal space (aortic area) and radiates to the carotid arteries. Patients with severe AS may also present with "pulsus parvus et tardus," which is characterized by a weak (parvus) and delayed (tardus) carotid upstroke. This is due to the increased afterload from the stenotic aortic valve, which causes the left ventricle to take longer to eject blood during systole. Therefore, the correct answer is (E) Aortic valve stenosis.

[PROMPT HIDDEN FOR CLARITY]
Error: >>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>> list index out of range
--------Reason--------

--------Better Prompt--------

Question: A 24-year-old woman presents to the office for evaluation of intermittent vertigo for the past 2 days. She complains of vomiting and complete hearing loss in her left ear. The patient believes she had an ear infection in the same ear but never sought out treatment. She has a past medical history of uterine fibroids. She takes mifepristone daily to reduce the size of the fibroids. Her blood pressure is 114/72 mm Hg, the heart rate is 68/min, the respiratory rate is 12/min, and the temperature is 36.8°C (98.2°F). The patient appears pale and in mild distress. Otoscopy reveals a suppurative, erythematous, bulging tympanic membrane. The tympanic membrane is observed to be immobile as confirmed by the pneumatoscope. Pure-tone audiometry testing reveals hearing loss across all frequencies in the left ear. Heart auscultation is without murmurs. Lungs are clear to auscultation bilaterally. Nystagmus is positive to the right. MRI of the brain does not reveal a tumor or abscess. Her physician orders a complete blood count with differential and comprehensive metabolic panel, which show:
Hemoglobin 14.5 gm/dL
Hematocrit 43.2 %
White blood cells 14,500 /mm3
Platelets 312,000/mm3
Neutrophil 81.6%
Lymphocyte 15.2%
Monocyte 1.4%
Eosinophil 1.2%
Basophil 0.6%
Serum Na+ 138 mEq/L
K+ 4.3 mEq/L
Cl- 108 mmol/L
BUN 12 mg/dL
Creatinine 0.8 mg/dL
Which of the following is the most appropriate antibiotic choice for this patient?(A) Ofloxacin
(B) Cefepime
(C) Cefotaxime
(D) Amoxicillin
(E) Cephalexin
gpt-4-32k Response: (D) Amoxicillin

This patient's presentation is consistent with acute otitis media (AOM), which is characterized by a rapid onset of ear pain and often associated with systemic symptoms (eg, fever, irritability) and signs of middle ear inflammation (eg, bulging tympanic membrane, reduced mobility with pneumatic otoscopy). AOM is most commonly caused by Streptococcus pneumoniae, Haemophilus influenzae, and Moraxella catarrhalis. First-line treatment for AOM in patients >6 months of age is amoxicillin. The high dose (80-90 mg/kg/day) is preferred because of its increased efficacy against resistant S pneumoniae. If the patient is allergic to penicillin, cefdinir, cefuroxime, or cefpodoxime is recommended. If the patient has vomiting or is dehydrated and unable to tolerate oral medication, parenteral ceftriaxone is recommended. If the patient fails initial therapy, amoxicillin-clavulanate or ceftriaxone is recommended.
--------------------------------------------------------------------------

Question: A 57-year-old woman comes to the physician because of a 3-month history of fatigue. She also reports generalized itchiness that started approximately 2 months ago. Two weeks ago, she noticed yellowing of her eyes. She does not smoke or drink alcohol. She uses eyedrops for persistent dryness of the eyes. Her vital signs are within normal limits. Physical examination shows jaundice of the conjunctivae and skin. The mucous membranes of the mouth are dry. The abdomen is soft. The liver edge is palpable 3 cm below the right costal margin. There are generalized skin excoriations. Laboratory studies show:
Hemoglobin 15 g/dL
Leukocyte count 7,700/mm3
Platelet count 332,000/mm3
Serum
Glucose 122 mg/dL
Total bilirubin 3.1 mg/dL
Direct 2.5 mg/dL
Alkaline phosphatase 452 U/L
AST 155 U/L
ALT 168 U/L
Hepatitis B surface antigen negative
Hepatitis B core IgM antibody negative
Hepatitis B surface antibody positive
Hepatitis C antibody negative
Ultrasonography of the abdomen shows mildly increased echogenicity of the liver. Which of the following additional findings is most likely to be found in this patient?"(A) Inferior vena cava thrombosis
(B) Cutaneous xanthomas
(C) Butterfly-shaped vertebrae
(D) Increased CA 19-9 levels
(E) Kayser-Fleischer rings
"
gpt-4-32k Response: (B) Cutaneous xanthomas

This patient's presentation of fatigue, pruritus, jaundice, dry eyes (sicca syndrome), and hepatomegaly is suggestive of primary biliary cholangitis (PBC), a chronic, progressive cholestatic liver disease. It is most common in middle-aged women and is often associated with other autoimmune conditions. The disease is characterized by destruction of the small intrahepatic bile ducts, leading to cholestasis, fibrosis, and ultimately cirrhosis. Cholestasis results in pruritus and jaundice and can lead to fat-soluble vitamin deficiencies, including vitamin D. This can cause osteoporosis and pathologic fractures, leading to vertebral compression fractures (butterfly vertebrae) and bone pain. Patients with PBC may also have hyperlipidemia, leading to xanthomas (deposits of cholesterol in the skin). The diagnosis of PBC is confirmed by the presence of antimitochondrial antibodies, which are present in 90%-95% of cases.

Question: A 35-year-old woman presents to the physician because of episodes of difficulty swallowing for the past 3 months. She feels solid food getting stuck in her chest behind the sternum. She does not have any issues with liquids. She has no coughing or nasal regurgitation. She has no hoarseness or weight loss. She reports occasional heartburn that has lasted for about a year. Her past medical history is significant for asthma and eczema. She has no history of any serious illness and takes no medications. Her vital signs are within normal limits. Physical examination shows no abnormal findings. An endoscopic image of the esophagus is shown. Mucosal biopsy shows eosinophilic infiltration. Which of the following is the most appropriate pharmacotherapy at this time?(A) Amlodipine
(B) Budesonide
(C) Fluconazole
(D) Nitroglycerin
(E) Omeprazole
gpt-4-32k Response: (E) Omeprazole

This patient's dysphagia, heartburn, and esophageal eosinophilia are suggestive of eosinophilic esophagitis (EoE), a chronic, immune/antigen-mediated esophageal disease characterized by symptoms related to esophageal dysfunction and eosinophil-predominant inflammation. Patients typically present with dysphagia, food impaction, and heartburn unresponsive to proton pump inhibitors (PPIs). EoE is commonly associated with other atopic diseases such as asthma, eczema, and allergic rhinitis, as seen in this patient. Diagnosis is confirmed by endoscopy with biopsy, which shows ≥15 eosinophils per high-power field. Initial treatment of EoE involves dietary modification and PPI therapy. Although EoE is a separate entity from gastroesophageal reflux disease, PPIs have been shown to improve symptoms and reduce esophageal eosinophilia in a subset of patients with EoE. Therefore, all patients with suspected EoE should receive a trial of high-dose PPI therapy. If symptoms persist after 8 weeks, swallowed glucocorticoids (eg, fluticasone, budesonide) should be added.

Question: 
gpt-4-32k Response: There are several ways to check if a number is prime in Python. Here is a simple method:

```python
def is_prime(n):
    if n <= 1:
        return False
    elif n <= 3:
        return True
    elif n % 2 == 0 or n % 3 == 0:
        return False
    i = 5
    while i * i <= n:
        if n % i == 0 or n % (i + 2) == 0:
            return False
        i += 6
    return True
```

In this function, we first handle the special cases of numbers less than or equal to 3. Then we check if the number is divisible by 2 or 3. If it is, it's not a prime number. 

For the rest of the numbers, we start from 5 and increment it by 6 each time (since all prime numbers are of the form 6k ± 1). We only need to check up to the square root of the number, because a larger factor of the number would be a multiple of smaller factor that has already been checked.

The function returns `True` if the number is prime and `False` otherwise.

Question: A four-day-old neonate is brought to the pediatrician with vaginal discharge for the last two days. Her mother is concerned about the blood-tinged discharge but states that her daughter has been feeding and voiding well. The neonate was delivered at 39 weeks gestation by an uncomplicated vaginal delivery, and she and her mother were discharged home after two days. The prenatal course was complicated by chlamydia in the mother during the first trimester, for which she and the partner were both treated with a negative test of cure. The neonate’s biological father is no longer involved the patient's care, but her mother’s boyfriend has been caring for the baby whenever the mother rests. At this visit, the neonate’s temperature is 98.5°F (36.9°C), pulse is 138/min, and respirations are 51/min. She appears comfortable, and cardiopulmonary and abdominal exams are unremarkable. There are no bruises or marks on her skin. Examination of the genitals reveals no vulvar irritation or skin changes, but there is scant pink mucoid discharge at the introitus. Which of the following is the best next step in management?(A) Vaginal exam under anesthesia
(B) Report to child protective services
(C) Vaginal culture
(D) Warm water irrigation of the vagina
(E) Reassurance
gpt-4-32k Response: (E) Reassurance

This neonate's blood-tinged vaginal discharge is a common and normal finding in female neonates. In utero, the fetus is exposed to high levels of maternal estrogens, which cause hypertrophy of the vaginal mucosa. After birth, the withdrawal of these hormones causes the mucosa to slough off, resulting in a bloody vaginal discharge (neonatal menstrual period). This usually occurs during the first week of life and can last for a few days. No treatment or further workup is necessary. Other findings of transient physiologic estrogenization in neonates can include breast enlargement in both genders and labial adhesions in females.

Administration of which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?(A) Sotalol
(B) Amiodarone
(C) Epinephrine
(D) Dopamine
(E) Atropine
gpt-4-32k Response: (B) Amiodarone

This patient's presentation of a tachycardic irregular rhythm with erratic undulations and no discernible P waves or QRS complexes is consistent with ventricular fibrillation (VF), a life-threatening arrhythmia that causes loss of consciousness and pulse within seconds. VF is typically triggered by an acute coronary syndrome, as in this patient. The first-line treatment for VF is immediate defibrillation. If the initial shock is unsuccessful, chest compressions should be initiated and an intravenous access should be established. Epinephrine, which has both α1-adrenergic (vasoconstrictive) and β-adrenergic (inotropic and chronotropic) effects, should be administered as soon as possible after VF is detected and every 3-5 minutes thereafter. If VF persists after the second shock, an antiarrhythmic agent (eg, amiodarone, lidocaine) should be administered. Amiodarone works by prolonging the action potential and refractory period and inhibiting adrenergic stimulation. It has been shown to increase survival to hospital admission when compared with lidocaine. After drug administration, rescuers should immediately resume chest compressions for 2 minutes before attempting another defibrillation.

Question: A 14-year-old girl comes to the physician with her father for evaluation of her short stature. She feels well overall, but is concerned because all of her friends are taller than her. Her birth weight was normal. Her father reports he had a short stature during his teenage years; he is currently 177 cm (5 ft 10 in) tall. She is at the 2ndpercentile for height and 35th percentile for weight. Breast development is Tanner stage 2. Pubic and axillary hair is absent. An x-ray of the left hand and wrist shows a bone age of 11 years. Which of the following is the most appropriate next best step in management?(A) Genetic karyotyping
(B) Pelvic ultrasound
(C) Measure serum dehydroepiandrosterone levels
(D) Reassurance and follow-up
(E) MRI of the brain
gpt-4-32k Response: (D) Reassurance and follow-up

This girl's short stature, delayed bone age, and delayed pubertal development are suggestive of constitutional delay of growth and puberty (CDGP), the most common cause of short stature and delayed puberty. CDGP is a variation of normal growth often seen in adolescents who are "late bloomers." These patients have delayed skeletal maturation, which means that they will continue to grow for a longer period than their peers and often reach a normal adult height. The condition is more common in boys and often runs in families, with one or both parents typically having had a similar growth pattern during adolescence. The patient's father's history of short stature during his teenage years supports this diagnosis. CDGP is a diagnosis of exclusion, but in a patient with normal birth weight, no dysmorphic features, and no chronic illnesses, it is appropriate to provide reassurance and continue to monitor growth and pubertal development. In contrast, genetic karyotyping (choice A) would be indicated in a patient with short stature and primary amenorrhea, especially if there were other features suggestive of Turner syndrome, such as webbed neck or broad chest. A pelvic ultrasound (choice B) would be indicated in a patient with primary amenorrhea and appropriate secondary sexual characteristics to evaluate for Müllerian anomalies. Measurement of serum dehydroepiandrosterone (DHEA) levels (choice C) would be indicated in a patient with signs of premature adrenarche, such as pubic hair, axillary hair, or body odor. Question: 
gpt-4-32k Response: A comma splice is a common grammatical error in English. It occurs when two independent clauses are joined together with a comma without a coordinating conjunction (for, and, nor, but, or, yet, so). 

For example: "I love ice cream, it's my favorite dessert." 

In this sentence, "I love ice cream" and "it's my favorite dessert" are both independent clauses that could stand alone as separate sentences. They are incorrectly joined together with a comma, creating a comma splice. 

To correct a comma splice, you could:
1. Separate the clauses into two sentences.
2. Use a coordinating conjunction.
3. Use a semicolon.
4. Use a subordinating conjunction.

Corrected examples:
1. "I love ice cream. It's my favorite dessert."
2. "I love ice cream, for it's my favorite dessert."
3. "I love ice cream; it's my favorite dessert."
4. "I love ice cream because it's my favorite dessert."

This is the space where you and the computer communicate and includes elements like the keyboard, mouse, screen, and software. There are two types of user interfaces: graphical user interfaces (GUIs) and command line interfaces (CLIs).

GUIs are the most common type of user interface today. They allow you to interact with your computer using graphics and icons, and they typically involve a mouse or touchscreen. Examples include Windows, macOS, and Ubuntu.

CLIs, on the other hand, require you to type text commands to interact with your computer. While this can be more difficult for beginners, it can also be more efficient for certain tasks. Examples include the Windows Command Prompt and the Unix Shell.

So, the difference between GUI and CLI is that GUIs use graphics and icons for you to interact with your computer, while CLIs require you to type text commands.

(C) Forearm pronation
(D) Index finger flexion
(E) Wrist extension
gpt-4-32k Response: (E) Wrist extension

The patient's symptoms of tingling and loss of sensation on the lateral side of the right forearm, along with the history of heavy weight lifting, suggest a radial nerve injury. The radial nerve innervates the extensor muscles of the forearm, which are responsible for wrist extension, finger extension, and thumb abduction. Therefore, an injury to the radial nerve would likely result in weakness of wrist extension.

Question: A 9-month-old boy is brought to the physician by his mother because of intermittent watery diarrhea for several months. Pregnancy and delivery were uncomplicated. He was diagnosed with eczematous dermatitis at 3 months old. His height and weight are below the 5th percentile. Immunologic evaluation shows a defect in activated regulatory T cells. (B) Ocular telangiectasias
(C) Autoimmune endocrinopathy
(D) Retained primary teeth
(E) Anaphylaxis to blood transfusions
gpt-4-32k Response: (C) Autoimmune endocrinopathy

This patient's chronic diarrhea, eczematous dermatitis, failure to thrive, and the presence of a FOXP3 gene mutation suggest a diagnosis of immune dysregulation, polyendocrinopathy, enteropathy, X-linked (IPEX) syndrome. IPEX is a severe disorder that manifests early in life with chronic diarrhea, type 1 diabetes mellitus, and eczematous dermatitis. Other autoimmune phenomena such as thyroiditis and hemolytic anemia can also occur. The FOXP3 gene normally functions to produce a transcription factor necessary for regulatory T-cell development. Regulatory T cells suppress immune responses, preventing autoimmunity. Mutations in FOXP3 impair the development of these cells, leading to uncontrolled immune activation and autoimmunity.

gpt-4-32k Response: In order to determine if a company is doing well, you need to consider several key performance indicators (KPIs). Here are some of the most important ones:

1. Revenue: This is the total amount of money a company earns before any expenses are subtracted. A company with growing revenue is usually doing well.

2. Net Income: This is the amount of money left after all expenses are subtracted from revenue. A company with a positive net income is making a profit.

3. Profit Margin: This is the percentage of revenue that is profit. The higher the profit margin, the more efficient the company is at turning revenue into profit.

4. Return on Investment (ROI): This measures how effectively a company uses its capital to generate profit. A high ROI means the company is doing well.

5. Market Share: This measures a company's sales in comparison to the total sales of all companies in the same industry. A company with a high market share is doing well.

6. Customer Satisfaction: This measures how happy customers are with a company's products or services. A company with high customer satisfaction is likely to have repeat customers and good word-of-mouth, which can lead to increased sales.

7. Employee Satisfaction: This measures how happy employees are with their jobs. A company with high employee satisfaction is likely to have lower turnover, which can lead to lower hiring and training costs.

8. Debt-to-Equity Ratio: This measures a company's financial leverage. A low debt-to-equity ratio indicates a company is using less debt to finance its operations, which is generally seen as a positive sign.

Remember, these indicators should be used in combination to get a comprehensive view of a company's performance. A company might have high revenue, for example, but if it also has high expenses and low customer satisfaction, it might not be doing as well as it seems.

Question: A 47-year-old man presents with hemoptysis, diffuse muscle pain, and shortness of breath. He has had these symptoms before but did not seek treatment. His past medical history is not known as he is not typically followed by a doctor and is homeless. His temperature is 99.0°F (37.2°C), blood pressure is 154/94 mmHg, pulse is 89/min, respirations are 22/min, and oxygen saturation is 90% on room air. Physical examination is notable for crackles noted in the bases of the lungs bilaterally. Labs values are ordered as seen below.

Serum:
Na+: 140 mEq/L
Cl-: 103 mEq/L
K+: 5.8 mEq/L
HCO3-: 21 mEq/L
BUN: 33 mg/dL
Glucose: 129 mg/dL
Creatinine: 2.6 mg/dL

Urine:
Color: Amber
Protein: Positive
Blood: Positive

Which of the following is the most appropriate treatment for this patient?(A) Methylprednisolone
(B) Methylprednisolone and cyclophosphamide
(C) Methylprednislone, IVIG, and cyclophosphamide
(D) Methylprednisolone, plasmapheresis, and cyclophosphamide
(E) Plasmapheresis and cyclophosphamide
gpt-4-32k Response: This patient's hemoptysis, diffuse muscle pain, shortness of breath, and pulmonary crackles, along with the presence of kidney disease (as indicated by elevated BUN and creatinine), suggest a diagnosis of vasculitis, specifically granulomatosis with polyangiitis (GPA, formerly Wegener's granulomatosis). GPA is a small-vessel vasculitis that commonly affects the upper airways, lungs, and kidneys. Constitutional symptoms such as fever, weight loss, and malaise are common. Pulmonary involvement can manifest with cough, hemoptysis, dyspnea, and chest discomfort. Renal involvement is typically rapidly progressive glomerulonephritis, which can manifest as hematuria, proteinuria, red cell casts, and elevated creatinine. The diagnosis is confirmed by tissue biopsy (eg, kidney, lung) showing granulomatous inflammation and necrotizing vasculitis, and/or positive serology for anti-neutrophil cytoplasmic antibodies (ANCA), specifically cytoplasmic ANCA (c-ANCA) or proteinase 3-ANCA. Treatment for severe GPA (eg, with renal or pulmonary involvement) is with glucocorticoids and cyclophosphamide (choice B). Rituximab is an alternative to cyclophosphamide. Plasmapheresis is added for patients with diffuse alveolar hemorrhage or severe renal disease (eg, serum creatinine >4 mg/dL).
--------------------------------------------------------------------------

This demonstrates an understanding of the disease and its management. The explanation provided in the output is thorough and accurate, explaining the reasoning behind the diagnosis and treatment choice.

(B) Get consent from the patient
(C) Obtain a court order for surgery
(D) Perform emergency laparotomy
(E) Delay surgery until parental consent
gpt-4-32k Response: (D) Perform emergency laparotomy

In this situation, it is ethically and legally acceptable to perform the surgery without explicit consent.

Question: A 46-year-old woman comes to the physician because of severe muscle and joint pain for 5 months. The pain varies in location and intensity. Strength and deep tendon reflexes are normal. Laboratory studies show:
Hemoglobin 14.3 g/dL
Leukocyte count 9300/mm3
Platelet count 230,000/mm3
Mean corpuscular volume 85 μm3
Erythrocyte sedimentation rate 12 mm/h
Serum
Glucose 92 mg/dL
Creatinine 0.6 mg/dL
TSH 3.1 μU/mL
Urinalysis is within normal limits. Which of the following is the most appropriate therapy for this patient?"(A) Bupropion
(B) Temazepam
(C) Vitamin B12
(D) Amitriptyline
(E) Methotrexate
"
gpt-4-32k Response: (D) Amitriptyline
